      T HE I MPLICIT B IAS OF S TRUCTURED S TATE S PACE
002   M ODELS C AN B E P OISONED W ITH C LEAN L ABELS

009                                                  A BSTRACT
010
011             Neural networks are powered by an implicit bias: a tendency of gradient descent
012             to fit training data in a way that generalizes to unseen data. A recent class of neu-
013             ral network models gaining increasing popularity is structured state space models
014             (SSMs), regarded as an efficient alternative to transformers. Prior work argued that
015             the implicit bias of SSMs leads to generalization in a setting where data is gener-
016             ated by a low dimensional teacher. In this paper, we revisit the latter setting, and
017
                formally establish a phenomenon entirely undetected by prior work on the implicit
                bias of SSMs. Namely, we prove that while implicit bias leads to generalization
018
                under many choices of training data, there exist special examples whose inclusion
019
                in training completely distorts the implicit bias, to a point where generalization
020             fails. This failure occurs despite the special training examples being labeled by
021             the teacher, i.e. having clean labels! We empirically demonstrate the phenomenon,
022             with SSMs trained independently and as part of non-linear neural networks. In
023             the area of adversarial machine learning, disrupting generalization with cleanly
024             labeled training examples is known as clean-label poisoning. Given the prolifera-
025             tion of SSMs, particularly in large language models, we believe significant efforts
026             should be invested in further delineating their susceptibility to clean-label poison-
027             ing, and in developing methods for overcoming this susceptibility.
028
029
030   1    I NTRODUCTION
031
032   Overparameterized neural networks can fit their training data in multiple ways, some of which gen-
033   eralize to unseen data, while others do not. Remarkably, when the training data is fit via gradient
034   descent (or a variant thereof), generalization tends to occur. This phenomenonРђћone of the greatest
035   mysteries in modern machine learning (Zhang et al. (2021); Chatterjee and Zielinski (2022))Рђћis
036   often viewed as stemming from an implicit bias: a tendency of gradient descent, when applied to
037
      neural network models, to fit training data in a way that complies with common data-generating
      distributions. The latter view was formalized for several neural network models and data-generating
038
      distributions (Soudry et al. (2018); Gunasekar et al. (2018); Razin et al. (2022); Neyshabur (2017)).
039
040   A recent class of neural network models gaining increasing popularity is structured state space mod-
041   els (SSMs). SSMs are often regarded as a computationally efficient alternative to transformers (Wang
042   et al. (2022)), and underlie prominent neural networks such as S4 (Gu et al. (2021)), Mamba (Gu
043
      and Dao (2023)), LRU (Orvieto et al. (2023)), Mega (Ma et al. (2023)), S5 (Smith et al. (2023))
      and more (Poli et al. (2023); Dao and Gu (2024)). The implicit bias of SSMs, i.e., of gradient
044
      descent over SSMs, was formally studied in prior works, e.g. Emami et al. (2021); Cohen-Karlik
045
      et al. (2022; 2023). Notable among these is Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023), which considered a setting
046   where data is generated by a low dimensional teacher SSM, and gradient flow (gradient descent
047   with infinitesimally small step size) applied to a high dimensional student SSM fits training data
048   comprising infinitely many sequences of a certain length.1 In this setting, the student SSM can fit
049   the training data in multiple ways, some of which generalize to sequences longer than those seen in
050   training, while others do not. It was shown in Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023) that under mild conditions,
051   an implicit bias leads to generalization.
052
          1
053         More precisely, the training data is formed from a continuous (Gaussian) distribution of sequences having
      a certain length, all labeled by the teacher SSM.


                                                            1
054
      In this paper, we revisit the setting of Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023), with one key exception: rather
055   than training data comprising infinitely many sequences, we consider the realistic case where the
056   number of sequences is finite. Surprisingly, our theory and experiments reveal a phenomenon en-
057   tirely undetected by prior works on the implicit bias of SSMs. Namely, we find that while implicit
058   bias leads the student SSM to generalize under many choices of sequences to include in training,
059   there exist special sequences which if included in training completely distort the implicit bias, re-
060   sulting in the student SSM failing to generalize. This failure to generalize takes place despite the
061   fact that the special sequences are labeled by the teacher SSM, i.e. they have clean labels! In the
062   area of adversarial machine learning, the phenomenon of generalization being disrupted by training
063
      instances with clean labels is known as clean-label poisoning, and received significant attention in
      recent years, both empirically (Huang et al. (2020); Shafahi et al. (2018)) and theoretically (Suya
064
      et al. (2021); Blum et al. (2021)). To our knowledge, the current paper is the first to formally prove
065
      susceptibility of SSMs to clean-label poisoning.
066
067   Our theoretical analysis comprises two main results, which may be of independent interest. First, is
068   a dynamical characterization of gradient flow over an SSM, trained individually or as part of a non-
069
      linear neural network. The dynamical characterization reveals that greedy low rank learning (Sun
      et al. (2021); Li et al. (2020); Razin et al. (2021; 2022))Рђћa sufficient condition for generalization
070
      with a low dimensional teacher SSMРђћis implicitly induced under many, but not all, choices of
071
      training sequences. Our second theoretical contribution builds on our dynamical characterization for
072   a fine-grained analysis of gradient flow over an SSM, employing an advanced tool from dynamical
073   systems theory: a non-resonance linearization theorem (Sell (1985)). The analysis proves that there
074   exist situations where: (i) training a student SSM on a collection of sequences labeled by a low
075   dimensional teacher SSM exhibits an implicit bias that leads to generalization; and (ii) adding to
076   the training set a single sequence, also labeled by the teacher SSM (i.e., that also has a clean label),
077   entirely distorts the implicit bias, to an extent where generalization fails.
078   We corroborate our theory via experiments, which demonstrate how adding a small amount of
079   cleanly labeled sequences to the training set of an SSM can completely ruin its generalization. In
080   light of the growing prominence of SSMs, particularly in the context of large language models, we
081   believe significant research efforts should be invested in further delineating their susceptibility to
082   clean-label poisoning, and in developing methods for overcoming this susceptibility.
083

      where for simplicity the dimension is omitted from the notation and should be inferred from context.

      Scalar series of finite lengths are identified with vectors.

      2.2   S TRUCTURED S TATE S PACE M ODELS
096
097   A structured state space model (SSM) of dimension d Рѕѕ N is parameterized by three matrices:
098   A Рѕѕ Rd,d , a state transition matrix, which conforms to a predefined structure (e.g. is constrained to
099   be diagonal); B Рѕѕ Rd,1 , an input matrix; and C Рѕѕ R1,d , an output matrix. Given the values of A,
100   B and C, the SSM realizes a mapping ¤Ћ(A,B,C) (┬и) that receives as input a length k scalar sequence
101   x Рѕѕ Rk , for any k Рѕѕ N, and produces as output a scalar y Рѕѕ R equal to the last element of the series
102   y Рѕѕ Rk defined through the following recursive formula:
103                             skРђ▓ = AskРђ▓ Рѕњ1 + BxkРђ▓ , ykРђ▓ = CskРђ▓ , k Рђ▓ Рѕѕ [k] ,                          (1)

      where (skРђ▓ Рѕѕ R )kРђ▓ Рѕѕ[k] is a sequence of states, and s0 = 0d . It is straightforward to show that
105                                                                       Рђ▓

108
      For convenience, we often identify an SSM with the triplet (A, B, C) holding its parameter matrices,
109   and regard the (single column) matrices B and C Ріц as vectors. Perhaps the most common form of
110   structure imposed on SSMs is diagonality (Gu et al. (2022); Gupta et al. (2022); Orvieto et al.
111   (2023); Ma et al. (2023); Gu and Dao (2023)). Accordingly, unless stated otherwise, we assume that
112   the state transition matrix A of an SSM is diagonal.
113
      Some of our results will account for SSMs that are part of non-linear neural networksРђћor more
114
      specifically, for SSMs whose output undergoes a transformation ¤Ѓ(┬и , w), where: ¤Ѓ : R ├Ќ W Рєњ R is
115   some differentiable mapping; W is some Euclidean space, regarded as a parameter space; and w Рѕѕ
116   W, regarded as a parameter vector. Given values for A, B, C and w, such a neural network realizes
117   the mapping ¤Ћ(A,B,C),w (┬и) := ¤Ѓ(¤Ћ(A,B,C) (┬и), w). This architecture (namely, an SSM followed by a
118   parametric transformation) is ubiquitous among SSM-based neural networks, for example Gu et al.
119   (2021), Gupta et al. (2022), and Gu et al. (2022).

151   hand, it was shown in Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023) that, regardless of the size of the training set S
152   and the input sequences it comprises (namely, (x(i) )ni=1 ), there exist assignments for (A, B, C) with
153   which the training loss РёЊ(┬и) is minimized and yet the student has arbitrarily high generalization error
154
      over sequence lengths beyond ╬║, e.g. ╬║ + 1 (for completeness, we prove this fact in Section A).
      The latter two facts together imply that if minimization of the training loss РёЊ(┬и) via gradient flow
155
      (Equation (4)) produces an assignment for (A, B, C) with which the student SSM generalizes, it
156
      must be a result of implicit bias. The main result in Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023) states that if the
157
      training set S is infinite and each entry of each input sequence x(i) is independently drawn from
158   the standard normal distribution (in other words, if the training loss РёЊ(┬и) is the expected value of
159   (y Рѕњ ¤Ћ(A,B,C) (x))2 , where the entries of x are independently drawn from the standard normal

      distribution and y = ¤Ћ(AРѕЌ ,B РѕЌ ,C РѕЌ ) (x)), then under mild conditions the implicit bias of gradient flow
163   is such that it convergences to a solution which generalizes over any sequence length k.
164
165   In this paper, we focus on the realistic case where the training set S is finite. Surprisingly, our theory
166
      and experiments (Sections 3 and 4, respectively) will reveal a phenomenon completely undetected
      by Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023), and any other work we are aware of on the implicit bias of SSMs.

169   3     T HEORETICAL A NALYSIS
170
171   In this section we present our theoretical analysis. For streamlining the presentation, we embed defi-
172   nitions and assumptions in the body of the text (rather than placing them in dedicated environments).
173   Readers who wish to view a concentrated list of all assumptions underlying each theoretical result

      are referred to Section B.

176   3.1     DYNAMICAL C HARACTERIZATION
177
      In this subsection we derive a dynamical characterization of gradient flow over an SSM, trained
178   individually or as part of a non-linear neural network. The dynamical characterization will reveal
179   that greedy low rank learning (Arora et al. (2019); Li et al. (2020); Razin et al. (2021; 2022))Рђћa
180   sufficient condition for generalization with a low dimensional teacher SSMРђћis implicitly induced
181   under many, but not all, choices of training sequences. Section 3.2 will build on the dynamical
182   characterization to prove that the implicit bias of SSMs can be poisoned with clean labels.
183
      Our dynamical characterization applies to a setting more general than that laid out in Section 2.3.
184   Namely, it applies to the same setting, with two exceptions: (i) the student SSM is potentially
185   embedded in a non-linear neural network, i.e. the mapping ¤Ћ(A,B,C) (┬и) is replaced by ¤Ћ(A,B,C),w (┬и)
186   as defined in Section 2.2; and (ii) the training labels (y (i) )ni=1 need not be assigned by a teacher
187   SSM, i.e. they may be arbitrary. We denote the resulting training lossРђћa generalization of РёЊ(┬и)
188   defined in Equation (3)Рђћby РёЊ╠Ѓ(┬и), namely:
189                                                  n

      Interpretation. Proposition 1 (Equations (8) to (11)) implies that during gradient flow, the motion
217   of aj (┬и)Рђћthe jРђЎth diagonal entry of the state transition matrix A(┬и)Рђћis given by a degree ╬║ Рѕњ 2
218   polynomial in aj (┬и), where the coefficients of the polynomial are time-varying. In particular, at time
219   t Рѕѕ R>0 of optimization, the coefficient of the lРђЎth power in the polynomial, for l Рѕѕ {0, 1, . . . , ╬║Рѕњ2},
220   is a product of two factors: (i) ╬│ (l) (t), which depends on the power l but not on the entry j; and
221   (ii) bj (t)cj (t)РђћjРђЎth entry of the input matrix B(┬и) times the jРђЎth entry of the output matrix C(┬и)Рђћ
222   which does not depend on the power l but does depend on the entry j. Consider the case where A(┬и)
223   emanates from standard near-zero initialization (Glorot and Bengio (2010); He et al. (2015); Par-
224   nichkun et al. (2024)), i.e. where aj (0) РЅѕ 0 for all j. If the factor ╬│ (0) (┬и) is small throughoutРђћas is
225   the case, e.g., if the ╬║ Рѕњ 1РђЎth entry of each training sequence x(i) is small (see Equation (9))Рђћthen
226   the constant term in the polynomial determining the motion of aj (┬и) is negligible. The dynamics of
227   (aj (┬и))dj=1 then exhibit greedy learning, similarly to the dynamics of various quantities in various
228   types of neural networks (Arora et al. (2019); Li et al. (2020); Razin et al. (2021; 2022)). Namely,
229
      (aj (┬и))dj=1 all progress slowly at first, following near-zero initialization, and then, whenever an entry
      reaches a critical threshold, it starts moving rapidlyРђћsee empirical demonstrations in Figure 2. The
230
      greedy learning of (aj (┬и))dj=1 implies a greedy low rank learning of the state transition matrix A.
231
      More specifically, it implies a tendency to fit training data with A having low rank, meaning a ten-
232   dency to generalize if data is generated by a low dimensional teacher SSM. In stark contrast, if the
233   training sequences (x(i) )ni=1 are such that the factor ╬│ (0) (┬и) is not small, then the polynomials deter-
234   mining the motions of (aj (┬и))dj=1 have non-negligible constant terms, and greedy low rank learning
235   will generally not take placeРђћsee empirical demonstrations in Figure 2 and Section F.1.
236
237   3.2        C LEAN -L ABEL P OISONING
238
239   Building on the dynamical characterization from Section 3.1, in this subsection we provide a fine-
240   grained analysis of gradient flow over an SSM. The analysis considers a teacher-student setting as
241   in Section 2.3, and proves existence of situations where: (i) training a student SSM on a collection
242   of sequences labeled by a low dimensional teacher SSM exhibits an implicit bias that leads to gen-
243
      eralization; and (ii) adding to the training set a single sequence, also labeled by the teacher SSM
      (i.e., that also has a clean label), entirely distorts the implicit bias, to an extent where generalization
244
      fails. To our knowledge, this constitutes the first formal proof of susceptibility of SSMs to clean
245
      label poisoning. Facilitating our analysis is an advanced tool from dynamical systems theoryРђћa
246   non-resonance linearization theoremРђћwhich may be of independent interest.
247
248   Hereinbelow we present our analysis in a basic setting, deferring more elaborate settings (all treated
249
      similarly) to Section E. Suppose the teacher SSM has dimension dРѕЌ = 2 and parameters:
                                                    Рѕџ                          Рѕџ

250                      РѕЌ     1 0                            Ріц
                                       , BРѕЌ = 1                   , CРѕЌ = 1

                      A =
                               0 0                    d Рѕњ  1                     d Рѕњ 1   .              (12)
251
252
      Suppose also that the state transition matrix of the student SSM A(┬и) emanates from standard near-
253
      zero initialization (Glorot and Bengio (2010); He et al. (2015); Parnichkun et al. (2024)), and its
254   input and output matrices B(┬и) and C(┬и) are fixed at 1d and 1Ріц         d , respectively. In this setting, a
255   sufficient condition for the student SSM to achieve low generalization error over all sequence lengths
256   (Definition 1) is that one of the diagonal entries of A(┬и) be close to one while the rest are close
257   to zero. Theorem 1 below shows that the latter sufficient condition for generalization is satisfied
258   under some choices of training sequences, and yet, despite the condition being mild, adding a single
259   sequence labeled by the teacher SSM (i.e., a single sequence that has a clean label) can entirely fail
260   generalization.
261   Theorem 1. Assume that the training sequence length and the dimension of the student SSM re-
262   spectively satisfy ╬║ Рѕѕ {7, 9, 11, . . .} and d РЅЦ 8. Let k Рѕѕ NРЅЦ╬║+2 and ¤х Рѕѕ R>0 . Then, for any n Рѕѕ N,
263   there exist a training set S = (x(i) , y (i) )ni=1 (where, for every i Рѕѕ [n], РѕЦx(i) РѕЦРѕъ = 1), a labeled se-
264   quence (xРђа , y Рђа ) Рѕѕ R╬║ ├Ќ R (where the entry of xРђа with largest absolute value is the second-to-last,
265   holding the value n1/2 ), and an open set I of initializations for the student SSM,3 such that, with
266
      any initialization in I, the following holds:
267               Рђб gradient flow converges to a point at which the training loss is minimal (i.e., equals zero)
268                 and the generalization errors over sequences lengths 1, 2, . . . , k are no greater than ¤х; and

      Proof sketch (proof in Section D). Figure 1 illustrates the main ideas behind the proof. Below is a

290
291   The proof shows that the student SSM admits low generalization error (over any sequence length)
292
      when its state transition matrix A has a single (diagonal) entry close to one and the remaining entries
      close to zero. That is, the set labeled Рђюgeneralizing solutionsРђЮ in Figure 1 comprises a neighborhood
293
      of one-hot assignments for A. Using the dynamical characterization from Proposition 1, the proof
294
      establishes that with a properly constructed training set S (namely, a training set S without se-
295   quences in which the last elements are relatively large4 ), if gradient flow emanates from standard
296   near-zero initialization, then it exhibits greedy low rank learning of A (see interpretation following
297   Proposition 1, as well as empirical demonstrations in Figure 2 and Section F.1), and accordingly
298   converges to a generalizing solution. Thus, under choices of I (set of initializations) close to the
299   origin, when training on S, a gradient flow trajectory emanating from I converges to the set of
300   generalizing solutionsРђћas illustrated in Figure 1.
301   To analyze the behavior of gradient flow when training on S Рѕф (xРђа , y Рђа ), the proof makes use of the
302   structure of xРђа (namely, the fact that its last elements are relatively large5 ) to show that greedy low
303   rank learning does not take place (see interpretation following Proposition 1, as well as empirical
304   demonstrations in Figure 2 and Section F.1). This allows identifying certain reference trajectories
305   that converge to non-generalizing solutions (one such reference trajectory is illustrated in Figure 1).
306   These reference trajectories emanate from initializations that cannot be included in I, since they
307   lead gradient flow to converge to non-generalizing solutions even when training on S. However, the
308   proof shows that I can comprise initializations near those of reference trajectories, since under such
309
      choice of I, each of its initializations leads gradient flow to: (i) converge to a generalizing solution
      when training on S; and (ii) closely track a reference trajectory when training on S Рѕф (xРђа , y Рђа ),
310
      resulting in convergence to a non-generalizing solutionРђћas illustrated in Figure 1. This concludes
311
      the proof.
312
313   The main technical challenge faced by the proof lies in item (ii) above, namely, in establishing that
314   when training on S Рѕф (xРђа , y Рђа ), an initialization near that of a reference trajectory leads gradient flow
315
      to closely track the reference trajectory. Since the training loss is non-convex, gradient flow tra-
      jectories can diverge from one another exponentially fast. Establishing that a reference trajectory is
316
      tracked thus requires sharp bounds on convergence times. The crux of the challenge is to derive such
317
      bounds, as trajectories pass near saddle points, and a-priori, may not escape (the vicinities of) these
318   saddle points sufficiently fast. To show that saddle points are escaped swiftly, the proof employs
319   an advanced tool from dynamical systems theory which may be of independent interest: a non-
320   resonance linearization theorem (Sell (1985)). Namely, rather than directly analyzing trajectories in
321                                                                              Pn
322

            For simplicity, the proof considers S = {pi ┬и e1 , pi }n
                                                                   i=1 , where
                                                                                       2
                                                                                  i=1 pi = n, but it is possible to account
323   for a much wider class of training setsРђћsee Section E for details.

            The proof takes xРђа = n 2 e╬║Рѕњ1 .

330
331   Figure 2: Demonstration of the dynamical characterization derived in Proposition 1Рђћoptimization of an SSM,
332   trained individually or as part of a non-linear neural network, implicitly induces greedy learning of the (di-
333   agonal) entries of the state transition matrix A under some, but not all, choices of training sequences. First
334   (leftmost) plot shows the magnitudes of the entries of A throughout the iterations of gradient descent, in a case
      where a student SSM of dimension d = 10 is trained individually on a training set labeled by a teacher SSM
335
      of dimension d = 1, and the training set does not include РђюspecialРђЮ sequences, i.e. sequences in which the last
336   elements are relatively large. Second plot portrays the exact same scenario, except that special sequences are
337   included in the training set. Third and fourth plots adhere to the descriptions of first and second plots, respec-
338   tively, except that the student SSM is trained along with a successive multi-layer perceptron (non-linear neural
339   network), and the teacher SSM is followed by a (fixed) multi-layer perceptron. Notice that, with and without
      a multi-layer perceptron, greedy learning takes place when special sequences are excluded, and does not take
340
      place when they are included. For further details and experiments (including teachers of higher dimension) see
341   Sections F.1 and G.1.
342
343
344   the vicinity of a saddle point, the proof constructs linear approximations, and uses the non-resonance
345   linearization theorem to show that the linear approximations are sufficiently accurate, which in turn
346   implies that the trajectories escape the saddle point sufficiently fast. The non-resonance lineariza-
347
      tion theorem requires the spectrum of the Hessian of the training loss to be free of certain algebraic
      dependencies known as resonances. If these resonances are absentРђћwhich the proof shows to be
348
      the caseРђћthe non-resonance linearization theorem provides guarantees on the accuracy of linear ap-
349
      proximations that are far better than guarantees attainable via standard smoothness arguments.
350
351
352   4                   E XPERIMENTS
353
354   This section presents experiments corroborating our theory. It is organized as follows. Section 4.1
355
      demonstrates the dynamical characterization we derived (in Proposition 1), showcasing that opti-
      mization of an SSM implicitly induces greedy low rank learning (a sufficient condition for general-
356
      ization with a low dimensional teacher SSM) under some, but not all, choices of training sequences.
357
      Section 4.2 then demonstrates the clean-label poisoning phenomenon we established (in Theorem 1),
358   by showing that adding a small amount of cleanly labeled sequences to the training set of an SSM
359   can completely ruin its generalization. Code for reproducing all of the experiments will be made
360   publicly available.
361
362   4.1                   DYNAMICAL C HARACTERIZATION
363
364   As discussed in Section 3.1, the dynamical characterization in Proposition 1 (Equations (8) to (11))
365   implies that optimization of an SSMРђћtrained individually or as part of a non-linear neural
366   networkРђћimplicitly induces greedy learning of the (diagonal) entries of the state transition ma-
367
      trix A under some, but not all, choices of training sequences. For example, if the penultimate entry
      of each training sequence is small then greedy learning takes place, and if these entries are not small
368
      then greedy learning may not take place. Figure 2 clearly demonstrates this, for a standalone SSM as
369
      well as one included in a non-linear neural network. Further experiments are reported in Section F.1.
370
371
      4.2                   C LEAN -L ABEL P OISONING
372
373   Theorem 1 proved existence of situations where clean-label poisoning of an SSM takes place, i.e. sit-
374   uations where: (i) training a student SSM on a collection of sequences labeled by a low dimensional
375   teacher SSM exhibits an implicit bias that leads to generalization; and (ii) adding to the training set
376   a sequence also labeled by the teacher SSM (i.e., that also has a clean label) entirely distorts the
377   implicit bias, to an extent where generalization fails. Table 1 demonstrates clean-label poisoning of
      SSMs in three different settings: the setting of Theorem 1; an SSM setting beyond Theorem 1 (i.e.,


                                                                                                                7
378
      an SSM setting that does not satisfy the assumptions of Theorem 1, e.g. it includes learning of the
379   input and output matrices B and C, respectively); and a setting where an SSM is part of a non-linear
380   neural network.
381
382
      We further demonstrate clean-label poisoning of SSMs in a real-world (non-synthetic) setting. To
      that end, we employ an adapted version of a method known as Gradient Matching (Geiping et al.,
383
      2020) for generating cleanly labeled examples that poison (i.e., that disrupt generalization for) an
384
      SSM-based S4 neural network (Gu et al., 2021) trained on the sequential MNIST dataset (Deng,
385   2012). In this experiment we do not have access to a teacher (i.e., to a ground truth labeling function),
386   and accordingly, a cleanly labeled poisonous example is generated from a given training example by
387   introducing human-imperceptible noise to the input sequence, while keeping the label intact. The
388   noise introduced for generating a cleanly labeled poisonous example has its last entries relatively
389   large, in line with our theory. After training with cleanly labeled poisonous examples as above, the
390   S4 neural network misclassifies preselected test instancesРђћsee Table 2.
391   We believe the susceptibility of SSMs to clean-label poisoning goes far beyond the demonstrations
392   herein. In light of the growing prominence of SSMs, particularly in the context of large language
393   models, further delineating this susceptibility, and developing methods for overcoming it, are of
394   prime importance.
395
396   Table 1: Demonstration of clean-label poisoning of SSMs in three different settings: the setting of Theorem 1;
397   an SSM setting beyond Theorem 1 (i.e., an SSM setting that does not satisfy the assumptions of Theorem 1,
398   e.g. it includes learning of the input and output matrices B and C, respectively); and a setting where an SSM
399
      is part of a non-linear neural network, i.e. is followed by a multi-layer perceptron. In each setting, a high
      dimensional student is trained until convergence (i.e., until the training loss is lower than 0.01), and data is
400   generated (i.e., sequences are labeled) by a low dimensional teacher of the same architecture as the student.
401   Reported are generalization errors (each averaged over 4 random seeds) for two training sets per setting: a
402   training set that does not include РђюspecialРђЮ sequences, i.e. sequences in which the last elements are relatively
403   large; and a training set that does include such sequences. In the first two settings (SSMs trained independently)
404
      generalization errors are measured via impulse responses, as defined in Definition 1. In the third setting (SSM
      trained as part of non-linear neural network) generalization errors are measured using a held-out test set. All
405   reported generalization errors were normalized (scaled) such that a zero mapping corresponds to a value of one.
406   Notice that across all settings, special training sequences significantly deteriorate generalization. For further
407   details and experiments (including teachers of higher dimension) see Sections F.2 and G.2.

415
416   5    R ELATED W ORK
417
418
      SSMs can be viewed as a special case of linear dynamical systems (LDSs)Рђћa classic object of study
419
      in areas such as systems theory (Oppenheim et al. (1996)) and control theory (Sontag (1990)). The
420   problem of learning from data an SSM that admits in-distribution and out-of-distribution generaliza-
421   tion is an instance of what is known in the LDS literature as system identification (Simpkins (2012)).
422   Determination of whether a high dimensional SSM realizes a mapping that is also realizable by a
423   low dimensional SSM (in our context, these are a student and a teacher, respectively) is considered
424   in the LDS literature under the topic of minimal realization theory (Silverman (1971)). Despite these
425   connections, our work is clearly distinct from classic LDS literature: it studies the implicit bias of
426   gradient descentРђћa phenomenon brought to light by the recent rise of overparameterized neural
427   networks (Neyshabur (2017)).
428   Several recent works formally studied the implicit bias of gradient descent in the context of recurrent
429   neural networks (Lim et al. (2021); Emami et al. (2021); Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023))Рђћa broad class
430   of models that includes SSMs. Some of these works, namely Emami et al. (2021); Cohen-Karlik
431   et al. (2023) focus specifically on SSMs, in particular Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023) which we extend
      (by lifting the unrealistic assumption of infinite training data). However, to our knowledge, none of


                                                              8
432   Table 2: Demonstration of clean-label poisoning of SSMs in a real-world (non-synthetic) setting. Each row in
433   the table summarizes an experiment using an adapted version of the Gradient Matching method for generat-
434   ing cleanly labeled examples that poison a four layer SSM-based S4 neural network trained on the sequential
435
      MNIST dataset. Each cleanly labeled poisonous example is generated from a given training example by intro-
      ducing human-imperceptible noise to the input sequence, while keeping the label intact. The noise introduced
436   for generating each cleanly labeled poisonous example has its last entries relatively large, in line with our the-
437   ory. The first column in the table specifies the number of test instances preselected for misclassification. The
438   second column indicates the percentage of cleanly labeled poisonous examples, i.e., of training examples sub-
439   ject to poisoning. The third and fourth columns present the number of test instances correctly classified before
440
      and after poisoning, respectively. The fifth column reports the size of the last elements of the noise in poisonous
      examples, quantified by the (Euclidean) norm of the last 3% of the elements in a noise sequence as a fraction
441   of the norm of the entire sequence, averaged across all poisonous examples. In all cases, the S4 neural network
442   achieved training accuracies exceeding 85%, with or without poisonous examples. For implementation details
443   see Section G.2.4.
444

446                        1                  10             1 out of 1          0 out of 1         0.898
447                        1                  1              1 out of 1          0 out of 1         0.602
448                        5                  5              5 out of 5          0 out of 5         0.981
449                        5                  1              5 out of 5          0 out of 5         0.841
450                       10                  1             9 out of 10         1 out of 10         0.744
451
452
453   the prior works on the implicit bias of gradient descent over SSMs of recurrent neural networks have
454   formally established susceptibility to clean-label poisoning, as we do.
455
      Since its demonstration in Shafahi et al. (2018), clean-label poisoning has received significant em-
456
      pirical attention (Huang et al. (2020); Zhu et al. (2019); Aghakhani et al. (2021); Zhao et al. (2020)).
457
      It was also studied theoretically for convex models in Suya et al. (2021); Blum et al. (2021).6 To the
458   best our knowledge, none of the prior works on clean-label poisoning have formally established the
459   phenomenon for SSMs, whose optimization results in a nonconvex objective.
460
461
      We note that the vast majority of literature (theoretical and empirical) on clean-label poisoning per-
      tains to classification problems, where the discontinuous nature of labels can be leveraged in favor of
462
      poisoning (e.g., training examples close to true decision boundaries can be used to distort the learned
463
      classifierРђЎs decision boundaries). In contrast, our work pertains to regression problems, where, ar-
464   guably, the continuous nature of labels renders it more challenging to establish clean-label poison-
465   ing.
466
467
468   6    L IMITATIONS
469
470   While this paper provides meaningful contributions to the understanding of the implicit bias of SSMs
471   and of clean-label poisoning, it is important to acknowledge several of its limitations. First, while
472   Theorem 1Рђћour theoretical result establishing susceptibility of SSMs to clean-label poisoningРђћis
473   extended in Section E, it is still an existence result that applies to specific settings. For example,
474   although it applies to a set of initializations that has positive volume, this volume may be low.
475
      Moreover, although it allows the input and output matrices to be learned, their learning rates must
      be small compared to that of the state transition matrix. Second, both Theorem 1 and our experi-
476
      ments pertain to near-zero initialization, and while such initialization is generally standard for neural
477
      networks (Glorot and Bengio (2010); He et al. (2015)), it does not account for modern SSM initial-
478   izations designed to alleviate vanishing gradients (Gu et al. (2020; 2022)). Third, due to vanishing
479   gradientsРђћwhich result in long run timesРђћall of our experiments have relatively low dimension for
480   the teacher SSM. Finally, while some of our theory treats SSMs trained as part of non-linear neural
481   networks, these non-linear neural networks do not account for various architectural features present
482   in modern SSM-based neural networks (e.g., multiple SSM layers as in S4 Gu et al. (2021), and
483
484       6
            Non-convex models were also studied theoretically, for example in Mahloujifar et al. (2019); Mahloujifar
485   and Mahmoody (2018); Gao et al. (2021), but these works considered a different type of poisoning, namely one
      where training examples are replaced (rather than added).


                                                              9
486
      selectivity as in Mamba Gu and Dao (2023)). Addressing the above limitations is regarded as an
487
      important set of directions for future research.
488
489
490   7    C ONCLUSION
491
492   The proliferation of SSMs, particularly in large language models, renders it crucial to understand
493   their implicit bias. In this paper, we revisited prior beliefs by which the implicit bias of SSMs leads
494   to generalization when data is generated by a low dimensional teacher. We formally proved and
495
      empirically demonstrated that, in stark contrast to these beliefs, there exist special examples whose
      inclusion in training completely distorts the implicit bias, to a point where generalization with a low
496
      dimensional teacher fails. This failure occurs despite the special training examples being labeled by
497
      the teacher, i.e. having clean labels!
498
499   Our results suggest significant challenges in both the theory and practice of SSMs. On the theoretical
500   front, our results suggest that generalization in SSMs cannot be explained via the traditional view of
501
      implicit complexity minimization (Yun et al. (2020); Soudry et al. (2018); Gunasekar et al. (2017)),
      or through the nascent view by which generalization is typical (Mingard et al. (2021; 2023); Buzaglo
502
      et al. (2024)). Indeed, if generalization in SSMs was due to the implicit bias finding a solution which,
503
      among all solutions fitting training data, minimizes some (data-independent) complexity measure,
504   then training with additional cleanly labeled examples would not change the solution found, and thus
505   would not disrupt generalization.7 Moreover, if generalization in SSMs was due to typicality, i.e.,
506   to the majority of solutions fitting training data being ones that generalize, then additional cleanly
507   labeled training examples would only improve generalization, as they enhance the dominance of
508   such majority. We believe fundamentally new approaches may be needed in order to theoretically
509   pinpoint the source of generalization in SSMs.
510   Moving to the practical side, the fact that generalization in SSMs can be disrupted by cleanly labeled
511   training examplesРђћi.e., that SSMs are susceptible to clean-label poisoningРђћraises significant con-
512   cerns regarding safety, robustness and reliability. For example, large language models, which are
513   becoming more and more reliant on SSMs (Glorioso et al. (2024); Pio╠Ђro et al. (2024); Alonso et al.
514   (2024)), are often fine-tuned via supervised learning on public internet data (Le Scao et al. (2023);
515   Touvron et al. (2023); Lhoest et al. (2021)), and in this process, it may be easy for a malicious ac-
516   tor to add cleanly labeled training examples, e.g., by adding unlabeled training examples prior to
517   label generation. We believe significant research efforts should be invested in further delineating
518
      the susceptibility of SSMs to clean-label poisoning, and in developing methods for overcoming this
      susceptibility.

      A     Z ERO LOSS , NON EXTRAPOLATING STUDENTS

758
      For completeness, we give a proof of Proposition 3 in Cohen-Karlik et al. (2023), which shows that
759   there exist student SSMs which generalize up to any given horizon k, but not up to a longer horizon.
760
761
      Lemma 1. Assume d > ╬║, and let c > 0 and q > k. Then, for any teacher (AРѕЌ , B РѕЌ , C РѕЌ ), there
      exists a d dimensional student (A, B, C) such that its generalization error over sequences of length

      k equals zero, and yet its generalization error over sequences of length q is > c.

      D     P ROOF OF T HEOREM 1

851
      In this section we prove Theorem 1. The outline of the proof is as follows; Section D.1 details the
852
      exact theoretical setting we consider. Section D.2 analyzes gradient flow over РёЊ on a dataset without
853   РђЮpoisonedРђЮ samples, and we show that it converges to a generalizing solution . Section D.3 analyzes
854   gradient flow after the addition of РђЮpoisonedРђЮ samples, showing that generalization is degraded.
855   Section D.4 proves that the different initialization sets considered in Section D.2 and Section D.3 in-
856   tersect, and that one can construct an open set I Ріє Rd such that both phenomena occur. Section D.5
857   contains auxiliary theorems and lemmas used throughout the proof.
858

1143
       D.3      G RADIENT FLOW OVER S2 CONVERGES BUT DOESN РђЎ T GENERALIZE
1144
1145   In this section we show that one can find a set of initialization I2 such that gradient flow under S2
1146   converges to a point with high generalization error. The proof shows that gradient flow trajecto-
1147   ries initialized in I2 evolve similarly to reference trajectories which provably stays away from any
1148   permutation of AРѕЌ .10 Since the training loss is non-convex, gradient flow trajectories can diverge
1149   from one another exponentially fast. Establishing that a reference trajectory is tracked thus requires
1150   sharp bounds on convergence times. The proof in this section is rather involved and is thus split into
1151
       several parts;
1152
                Рђб D.3.1 defines the reference trajectories and shows their poor ability of generalization.
1153
1154            Рђб D.3.2 characterizes the critical points of the objective РёЊ, focusing on a specific saddle point
1155              of interest (which we denote s).
1156            Рђб D.3.3 presents relevant background on dynamical systems, introducing a linearization re-
1157              sult needed for the rest of the proof.
1158
1159
                Рђб In D.3.4 we start analyzing the trajectories itself, showing that it must pass near s.
1160            Рђб D.3.5 shows that the trajectories must escape sufficiently fast from s using the tools pre-
1161              sented in D.3.3.
1162
                Рђб D.3.6 proves that after escaping from s the trajectories converge to global minima.
1163
1164            Рђб D.3.7 shows that the overall divergence between trajectories emanating from I2 and their
1165              corresponding reference trajectories can be bounded from above, implying the former tra-
1166              jectories have poor generalization.
1167
       Throughout this part, we omit the dependence on S2 for simplicity.
1168
1169
1170   D.3.1      R EFERENCE TRAJECTORIES
1171
       We begin our proof by proving the following useful lemma which states that gradient flows maintains
1172   the order of the entries of A:
1173
1174   Lemma 3. Suppose we initialize at A(0) Рѕѕ Rd and evolve A(t) according to Equation (28). Let

       ¤ђ : [d] Рєњ [d] be a permutation such that for any j Рѕѕ [d Рѕњ 1]:

                                   m
1186                                                k=1                               k=1
1187
         10
              Any permutation of AРѕЌ yields a system with the same impulse response.

                                                m

1276   Lemma 4 motivates us to find an open subset of initializations under which the respective gradient
1277   flow trajectories remain close to their reference trajectory counterparts, as this would allow us to

1661
       D.3.3    L INEARIZATION OF DYNAMICAL SYSTEMS
1662
1663   In D.3.2 we characterized the critical point s Рѕѕ 1d and established that it is the only non global
1664   minimum that we could converge to given our initialization. We would now like to show that in fact
1665   gradient flow will escape s and converge rapidly towards a global minimum. Corollary 2 gives some
1666   indication why this may be the case - it shows that the local linearization of the dynanics near s will
1667   tend to repel any trajectory which is not on the line W1 . Intuitively one expects that once we are
1668   sufficiently close to s, the linearized dynamics provide a sufficiently good approximation to ensure
1669
       that the same conclusion will hold for the nonlinear system as well. Unfortunately, existing results
       from the optimization literature (e.g. Jin et al. (2017)) give escape times which do not suffice for
1670
       our purposes11 . To obtain the required bounds on the escape time we will require some results from
1671
       dynamical systems theory. Informally, the idea is that if a non linear dynamical system satisfies
1672
          11
1673         recall that our strategy is to bound the divergence between our trajectory and the reference one, and this
       divergence depends on the convergence time achieved by gradient flow.


                                                             31
1674
       certain conditions on the spectrum of its linearization (these are sometimes called РђЮnon-resonance
1675   conditionsРђЮ), then it is locally smoothly equivalent to its linearization. This will allow us to bound
1676   the escape time of gradient flow in terms of the closed form dynamics obtained for the linearization
1677   in Corollary 2.
1678
       We begin by defining the notions of smooth conjugation and smooth linearization of dynamical
1679
       systems:
1680
1681   Definition 5. Let f, g : Rd Рєњ Rd be two C M vector fields with a common fixed point x0 Рѕѕ Rd ,
1682
       i.e., f (x0 ) = g(x0 ) = 0d . For any K Рѕѕ [M ] we say that f and g are C K -conjugate near x0 when
       there exist neighborhoods V1 , U1 Ріє Rd such that x0 Рѕѕ V1 , U1 and there exist a C K -diffeomorphism
1683
       H : V1 Рєњ U1 satisfying the following:
1684
1685          Рђб H(x0 ) = x0
1686
1687          Рђб Whenever x(t) Рѕѕ V1 is a solution of x╠Є(t) = f (x(t)) for t in some interval I Ріє R then
1688
                y(t) = H(x(t)) is a solution of y╠Є(t) = g(y(t)) for t Рѕѕ I.
1689          Рђб Whenever y(t) Рѕѕ U1 is a solution of y╠Є(t) = g(y(t)) for t in some interval I Ріє R then
1690            x(t) = H Рѕњ1 (y(t)) is a solution of x╠Є(t) = f (x(t)) for t Рѕѕ I.
1691
1692   The mapping H is referred to as the C K -conjugation between x╠Є(t) = f (x(t)) and y╠Є(t) = g(y(t)).
1693   Consider the first order TaylorРђЎs expansion of f around x0 given by
1694                             x╠Є(t) = f (x(t)) = A(x(t) Рѕњ x0 ) + F (x(t) Рѕњ x0 )
1695
1696
       where A = Df (x0 ), F (0d ) = 0d and DF (0d ) = 0d├Ќd . The associated linear equation is given by
1697                                            y╠Є(t) = A(y(t) Рѕњ x0 )
1698
1699
       We say that f admits a C K -linearization near x0 when it is C K -conjugate near x0 with its linear
       approximation.
1700
1701   We now introduce the Strict Hyperbolicity property and the Non-resonance condition (also known as
1702   the Sternberg condition). These are sufficient conditions for a dynamical system to admit a smooth
1703   linearization and we will later show our system satisfies them:
1704   Definition 6. Let A Рѕѕ Rd├Ќd be a matrix with eigenvalues ╬╗1 , . . . , ╬╗d Рѕѕ R repeated with multiplici-
1705   ties. We say that A is strictly hyperbolic when:
1706
1707          Рђб For all j Рѕѕ [d] it holds that ╬╗j ╠И= 0.
1708
              Рђб There exist j+ , jРѕњ Рѕѕ [d] such that ╬╗j+ > 0 and ╬╗jРѕњ < 0.
1709
1710   Definition 7. Let A Рѕѕ Rd├Ќd be a matrix with eigenvalues ╬╗1 , . . . , ╬╗d Рѕѕ R repeated with multiplic-
1711   ities. For any m Рѕѕ NdРЅЦ0 non-negative integers vector and any ╬╗ Рѕѕ R we denote ╬│(╬╗, m) as the
1712   following quantity:
1713                                                          d
                                                              X
1714                                        ╬│(╬╗, m) := ╬╗ Рѕњ          mk ┬и ╬╗k
1715                                                          k=1
1716   For any N Рѕѕ N such that N РЅЦ 2 we say that A satisfies the non-resonance condition of order N
1717                                                  Pd
       when for all j Рѕѕ [d] and all m Рѕѕ NdРЅЦ0 such that k=1 mk Рѕѕ {2, . . . , N } it holds that ╬│(╬╗j , m) ╠И= 0.
1718
1719   Finally, we present the property of matrix Q-smoothness:
1720   Definition 8. Let A Рѕѕ Rd├Ќd be a matrix with eigenvalues ╬╗1 , . . . , ╬╗d Рѕѕ R repeated with multiplici-
1721   ties. Suppose A is strictly hyperbolic. Denote the following quantities:
1722
1723
                           max{|╬╗j | : j Рѕѕ [d], ╬╗j > 0}         max{|╬╗j | : j Рѕѕ [d], ╬╗j < 0}
                   ¤Ђ+ :=                                , ¤ЂРѕњ :=
1724                       min{|╬╗j | : j Рѕѕ [d], ╬╗j > 0}         min{|╬╗j | : j Рѕѕ [d], ╬╗j < 0}
1725   Let Q Рѕѕ N>0 . We define the Q-smoothness of A to be the largest integer K Рѕѕ NРЅЦ0 for which there
1726   exist M, N Рѕѕ N>0 satisfying the following:
1727
              Рђб Q=M +N


                                                         32
1728
                 Рђб M Рѕњ K¤Ђ+ РЅЦ 0
1729
1730             Рђб N Рѕњ K¤ЂРѕњ РЅЦ 0
1731
1732   We are now ready to present Theorem 1 of Sell (1985), which states conditions under which there
1733
       exists a smooth linearization of a dynamical system12 :
1734   Theorem 3 (Theorem 1 of Sell (1985) (adapted)). Let Q Рѕѕ N such that Q РЅЦ 2. Let f : Rd Рєњ Rd be
1735   an analytic (i.e. C Рѕъ ) vector field with a fixed point x0 . Consider the first order TaylorРђЎs expansion
1736
       of f around x0 given by

3477   throughout training); and (iv) the training set S varies. We also account for limitations of the above
3478   extensions.

       Varying teacher input and output matrices. Given any teacher SSM (AРѕЌ , B РѕЌ , C РѕЌ ) with which
       Theorem 1 holds (including a high dimensional teacher as described above), a similar result holds
3496
       with the teacher SSM (AРѕЌ , ╬▒1 B РѕЌ , ╬▒2 C РѕЌ ), where ╬▒1 , ╬▒2 Рѕѕ R╠И=0 are arbitrary. Indeed, if we likewise
3497
       scale the values of the (fixed) student parameters B and C, i.e. we replace B by ╬▒1 B and C by ╬▒2 C,
3498   then for every sequence x:
3499
3500                               ¤Ћ(AРѕЌ ,╬▒1 B РѕЌ ,╬▒2 C РѕЌ ) (x) = ╬▒1 ╬▒2 ¤Ћ(AРѕЌ ,B РѕЌ ,C РѕЌ ) (x)
3501
       and likewise:
3502
                                      ¤Ћ(A,╬▒1 B,╬▒2 C) (x) = ╬▒1 ╬▒2 ¤Ћ(A,B,C) (x) .
3503
3504   The training loss and its derivatives thus scale by a positive factor, and so do generalization errors
3505   (Definition 1). Accordingly, the proof of Theorem 1 carries through.
3506
3507   Learned student input and output matrices. Below we outline a modification of Theorem 1 that
3508   accounts for a setting in which the input and output matrices of the student SSM are learned. Suppose
3509   these input and output matricesРђћB and C, respectivelyРђћare learned with a learning rate (step size)
       that may be different from the learning rate of the studentРђЎs state transition matrix A. Formally,


                                                            65
3510
       suppose the optimization trajectory (A(┬и), B(┬и), C(┬и)) is governed by the following dynamics:
3511
3512                                        Рѕѓ
                                A╠Є(t) = Рѕњ     РёЊ(A(t), B(t), C(t); S)
3513                                       РѕѓA
3514                                           Рѕѓ
                                B╠Є(t) = Рѕњ╬и ┬и      РёЊ(A(t), B(t), C(t); S) , t Рѕѕ RРЅЦ0 ,                       (32)
3515                                          РѕѓB
3516                                           Рѕѓ
                                C╠Є(t) = Рѕњ╬и ┬и      РёЊ(A(t), B(t), C(t); S)
3517                                          РѕѓC
3518   where ╬и > 0 represents the ratio between the learning rate of B and C, and the learning rate of A.
3519   Consider a trajectory induced by Equation (32), and a corresponding trajectory that emanates from
3520   the same initialization, but where only A is learned (or equivalently, where ╬и in Equation (32) is
3521   replaced by zero). Arguments similar to those used in the proof of Theorem 1 can be used to show
3522   that the divergence between these two trajectories is upper bounded by a quantity that depends on ╬и,
3523   and in particular tends to zero as ╬и does. Accordingly, if ╬и is sufficiently small, generalization errors
3524
       attained when A, B and C are learned jointly (i.e., when optimization is governed by Equation (32)),
       are close to those attained when only A is learned. Theorem 1Рђћwhich applies to a setting where
3525
       only A is learnedРђћthus translates to a result that applies to a setting where B and C are also learned.

       Varying training set. Theorem 1 proves existence of a specific training set S under which gradient
3528   flow converges to a generalizing solution. As we show below, one can extend this result to a much
3529   larger class of training sets.

       Theorem 5. Consider the teacher SSM given by

3582
       Limitations. While the abovedescribed extensions of Theorem 1 broaden its scope, they still entail
3583   limitations which are important to acknowledge. In general, Theorem 1 is an existence result, and
3584   even under the extensions above, it applies to specific settings. More specifically, it does not account
3585   for: large initializations and large learning rates (for A(┬и), and even more so for B(┬и) and C(┬и)); many
3586   values for the teacher parameters (AРѕЌ , B РѕЌ , C РѕЌ ); non-diagonal SSMs; and more. Further extending
3587   Theorem 1 is regarded as an important direction for future research.

       F     F URTHER E XPERIMENTS
3590
3591   F.1   DYNAMICAL C HARACTERIZATION
3592
3593   In Section 4.1 we provided experiments that corroborate the implication of the dynamical character-
3594   ization presented in Section 3.1 and demonstrate the implicit bias to greedy low rank learning of the
3595   state transition matrix A under some, but not all, choices of training sequences. In this appendix we
3596   report additional experiments, including other settings, that demonstrate this phenomenon. Figure 3
3597
       extends the experiments reported in Figure 2 to longer training sequences. Figures 4 to 6 showcase
       similar experiments to the ones in Figure 2, where the training sequences are labeled by teachers of
3598
       higher ranks. Figures 7 and 8 report the results achieved with different random seeds in the settings
3599
       of Figures 2 and 3, respectively. A classical continuous surrogate for the matrix rank is the effective
3600   rank (Roy and Vetterli, 2007). Figures 9 to 15 present the effective rank of the transition matrix A
3601   throughout optimization in the settings of Figures 2 to 8, respectively, underlining the low effective
3602   rank caused by greedy low rank learning. Finally, Figures 16 to 19 report the values of ╬│ (0) (t) (as
3603   defined in Proposition 1) observed during optimization in the settings of Figures 2, 3, 7, and 8, re-
3604   spectively, showcasing that larger absolute values of ╬│ (0) (t) do not correspond to greedy low rank
3605   learning, whereas lower absolute values do.
3606
3607   F.2   C LEAN -L ABEL P OISONING
3608
3609   In Section 4.2 we provided experiments which corroborate our theory in Section 3.2 and emphasize
3610   the potential generalization failures SSMs are susceptible to when adding special training sequences.
3611   In this appendix we report additional experiments demonstrating this phenomenon. Table 3 demon-
3612
       strates clean-label poisoning of SSMs in the same settings as the ones in Table 1, except that the
       sequences used to train the models were longer.

3699     Data. In all experiments we used the respective teachers to generate the labels for training se-
3700     quences. Additionally, we used training sequences of one of two types (РђЮbaselineРђЮ vs. РђЮspecialРђЮ),
3701     where each type had designated indices of non-zero entries. Table 4 specifies which non-zero in-
3702     dices were present in each sequence type for each experiment. Training sequences of both types had
3703     their non-zero entries sampled i.i.d from N (0, 1). Table 5 specifies how many training sequences of
3704
         each type were used in each experiment.
3705     Initialization. In all experiments we initialized the studentРђЎs A, B and C parameter matrices in a
3706     manner that was inspired by the initialization set I of Theorem 1.
3707
         To initialize (the diagonal) A in each experiment we first sampled d i.i.d entries from N (0, sd A),
3708     took their absolute values and then arranged them in descending order. Then, we set the second
3709     entry to have the first entryРђЎs value minus a constant diff. This was done to reflect the near zero
3710     initialization on the one hand and the proximity to the reference trajectory on the other hand. In the
3711     experiments reported in Figures 5 and 6 we naturally extended this procedure by setting the third
3712     entry to have the first entryРђЎs value minus 1.01 ┬и diff in both experiments, and the fourth entry to
3713     have the first entryРђЎs value minus 1.05 ┬и diff in the latter. Table 6 report the values of sd A and
3714     diff used in each experiment.
3715     To initialize B in each experiment we first sampled d i.i.d entries from N (0, sd B C), took their
3716     absolute values and then arranged them in descending order. Then, we set the second entry to have
3717     the first entryРђЎs value minus a constant diff. This was done to reflect the near zero initialization
3718     on the one hand and the proximity to the reference trajectory on the other hand. In the experiments
3719     reported in Figures 5 and 6 we naturally extended this procedure by setting the third entry to have
3720     the first entryРђЎs value minus 1.01 ┬и diff in both experiments, and the fourth entry to have the first
3721     entryРђЎs value minus 1.05 ┬и diff in the latter. To initialize C we followed the same procedure,
3722
         without modifying the second to potentially fourth entries. Note that in the experiments reported
         in Figures 4 to 6 the input and output matrices B(┬и) and C(┬и) were not trained. Table 6 report the
3723
         values of sd B C used in each experiment.
3724
3725     Optimization. In all of the experiments we trained using the empirical mean squared error as a loss
         function and optimized over full batches of the training sets. In order to facilitate more efficient

         experimentation in the experiments where a standalone SSM is trained, we optimized using gradient

3748
         by the square root of an exponential moving average of squared gradient norms (see appendix D.2
3749     in Razin et al. (2022) for more details). We used a weighted average coefficient of ╬▓ = 0.8 and a
3750     softening constant of 10Рѕњ6 . Note that only the learning rate (step size) is affected by this scheme,
3751     not the direction of movement. Comparisons between the adaptive scheme and optimization with a
3752     fixed learning rate showed no significant difference in terms of the dynamics, while run times of the
3753     former were considerably shorter. Table 7 specifies the base learning rate used in each experiment.

         In this Appendix we provide implementation details for the experiments provided in Sections 4.2
3815     and F.2. All synthetic experiments were implemented using Keras (Chollet et al., 2015) and were
3816     carried out using a single Nvidia RTX 2080 Ti GPU. The real-world experiments reported in Table 2
3817     were implemented using PyTorch (Paszke et al., 2019) and were carried out using a cluster of 8
3818     Nvidia RTX 2080 Ti GPUs.
3819
3820     G.2.1                 SSM P ER T HEOREM 1
3821
3822
         The main goal of the experiments in the first poisoning setting (standalone SSM per Theorem 1) was
         to approximate the solution to the system of ODEs induced by gradient flow (Equation (4)) in order
3823
         to demonstrate the results of Theorem 1. To do so we employed the use of the odeint function
3824
         of SciPy (Virtanen et al., 2020) which is a numerical solver for systems of ODEs based on lsoda
3825     from the FORTRAN library odepack (Hindmarsh, 1983). odeintРђЎs arguments are the initial point
3826     in parameter space A(0), the timestamps at which the solution is required, and a function which
3827     specifies the system by intaking a timestamp t and a point in parameter space A and outputting the
3828     derivative in time t at A. odeint outputs a set of numerical approximations for the solution of the
3829     system at the required timestamps.
3830
         Models. We use teacher and student models according to the setting described in Section 3.2. We
3831     used a teacher SSM of dimension dРѕЌ = 2 that was set with the parameters

         We used student SSM models whose input and output matrices B(┬и) and C(┬и) were fixed at 1d and
3856
         1Ріц
          d respectively. The student models had dimension d = 10 in the original poisoning experiments
3857
         (Table 1), and dimension d = 20 when training over longer sequences (Table 3).
3858
3859     Data. In all experiments we used the respective teachers to generate the labels for all training
3860     sequences. We trained using 5 РђЮbaselineРђЮ sequences of the form x ┬и e1 Рѕѕ Rk and a single РђЮspecialРђЮ
3861
         sequence of the form x ┬и ekРѕњ1 Рѕѕ Rk . Given some positive scalar P we determined the РђЮbaselineРђЮ
         sequences by first sampling 5 i.i.d values from N (0, 1), and then scaling
                                                                                 Рѕџ them such that their sum
3862
3863
         of squares is equal P . The single РђЮspecial sequenceРђЮ was set with x = P . This is done to satisfy
         a technical requirement of Theorem 1 (See Section D for details on this requirement). The reported
3864
         results use P = 10, and we saw similar results qualitatively when using other positive values for
3865
         P and other amounts of РђЮbaselineРђЮ and РђЮspecialРђЮ sequences. The relevant experiments reported in
3866     Tables 1 and 3 were trained using sequences of lengths 7 and 9 respectively.
3867
3868     Initialization. We initialized the studentРђЎs diagonal matrix A in a manner that was inspired by
3869
         the initialization set I of Theorem 1. In each experiment we first sampled d i.i.d entries from
         N (0, sd A) and then arranged them in descending order. We then set the second entry to have the
3870
         firstРђЎs value minus a constant diff. This was done to reflect the near zero initialization on the one
3871
         hand and the proximity to the reference trajectory on the other hand. Table 8 reports the values of
3872     sd A and diff used in each experiment.
3873
3874     Optimization. We input odeint a custom function which computes РѕњРѕЄРёЊ(A) (Equation (4)) given
3875
         the point A to be used for derivative computations. Table 9 reports the timestamps simulated in each
         experiment. All experiments reached training loss values of less than 10Рѕњ5 and stable generalization
3876
         errors.
3877
3878     Generalization evaluation. In the first poisoning setting (standalone SSM per Theorem 1) general-
3879     ization errors were measured via impulse responses of length 40 as defined in Definition 1, divided
3880     by the РёЊРѕъ norm of the teacherРђЎs length 40 impulse response (this normalization is chosen so that the
3881
         zero mapping has generalization error 1).

              Data. Data for the experiments were generated in the same manner as in Section G.1.1. Table 10
3909

3910

3913          Initialization. To speed up optimization we modified the initialized employed in Section G.1.1
3914
              by adding a small constant of 10Рѕњ1 to all entries of A and B at initialization. This modification
              showed no significant differences in terms of the generalization error achieved by the models when
3915

3916

3921          we report were stable, we trained for additional iterations after reaching sub 0.01 training loss. We
3922          trained the standalone SSM models for 1500 more iterations, and the models with additional layers
3923          for 5000 more iterations.

3925          40 as defined in Definition 1, divided by the РёЊРѕъ norm of the teacherРђЎs length 40 impulse response

3927          the original experiments (Table 1) and in the longer experiments (Table 3).

3977           G.2.4                       SSM IN R EAL -W ORLD S ETTING
3978
              The SSM-based S4 neural network adheres to the implementation provided in https://github.
3979          com/state-spaces/s4, utilizing the Рђюminimalist S4РђЮ configuration available in s4d.py and
3980          s4.py. In all experiments, the S4 neural network had four layers with a hidden dimension
3981          of 256. For the poisoning process, we adapted the Gradient Matching method provided in https:
3982          //github.com/JonasGeiping/poisoning-gradient-matching. The adaptation in-
3983          volved: reshaping the MNIST image input into a sequence format compatible with the S4 architec-
3984          ture; and introducing regularization that encourages the last elements of an injected noise sequence
3985          to be relatively large.14 Following this adaptation, apart from varying the number of target test in-
3986          stances and the percentage of poisonous examples, all hyperparameters were kept at their default
3987
              values.
3988
3989
3990
3991
3992
3993
3994
                 14
3995                Regularization comprised weight decay of 28 Рѕњ i applied to the noise entries corresponding to the ith row
              of an input image, where i Рѕѕ [28] (recall that MNIST images are of size 28 ├Ќ 28).
